In this chapter, we will delve into the world of thermodynamic processes. Thermodynamics is the branch of physics that deals with the study of energy and its transformations. # Thermodynamics & Kinetics: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 12: Thermodynamic Processes



### Section 12.1: Isothermal Process



An isothermal process is a type of thermodynamic process in which the temperature of a system remains constant. This means that the change in temperature, denoted as ΔT, is equal to zero. Isothermal processes are typically observed when a system is in contact with an outside thermal reservoir, allowing for continuous heat exchange to maintain a constant temperature. In contrast, an adiabatic process occurs when a system exchanges no heat with its surroundings.



## Etymology



The term "isothermal" is derived from the Greek words "ἴσος" ("isos") meaning "equal" and "θέρμη" ("therme") meaning "heat". This accurately reflects the nature of an isothermal process, where the temperature remains constant throughout.



## Examples



Isothermal processes can occur in a variety of systems, including highly structured machines and living cells. In fact, some parts of the cycles of heat engines, such as the Carnot cycle, are carried out isothermally. In the thermodynamic analysis of chemical reactions, it is common to first consider the behavior under isothermal conditions and then examine the effects of temperature. Phase changes, such as melting or evaporation, also occur isothermally when they take place at constant pressure. Isothermal processes are often used as a starting point for analyzing more complex, non-isothermal processes.



Ideal gases are of particular interest in isothermal processes due to Joule's second law, which states that the internal energy of an ideal gas is solely dependent on its temperature. This means that in an isothermal process, the internal energy of an ideal gas remains constant. This is a result of the absence of intermolecular forces in ideal gases. It is important to note that this only applies to ideal gases, as the internal energy of liquids is also dependent on pressure.



### Subsection 12.1a: Definition of Isothermal Process



An isothermal process is defined as a thermodynamic process in which the temperature of a system remains constant. This means that the change in temperature, ΔT, is equal to zero. In order for an isothermal process to occur, the system must be in contact with an outside thermal reservoir, allowing for continuous heat exchange to maintain a constant temperature. This process is often used as a starting point for analyzing more complex, non-isothermal processes.



Mathematically, an isothermal process can be represented by the equation:



$$

\Delta T = 0

$$



This equation shows that the change in temperature is equal to zero, indicating that the temperature remains constant throughout the process. This is a fundamental property of an isothermal process and is essential in understanding its behavior.



In an isothermal process, the internal energy of an ideal gas remains constant. This is due to the absence of intermolecular forces in ideal gases, which means that the internal energy is solely dependent on temperature. This property is described by Joule's second law and is a key factor in the behavior of ideal gases in isothermal processes.



Overall, an isothermal process is an important concept in thermodynamics and is applicable in various fields such as chemistry, engineering, and biology. It is a fundamental process that helps us understand the behavior of matter and energy in different systems. In the next section, we will explore the properties and applications of isothermal processes in more detail.





# Thermodynamics & Kinetics: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 12: Thermodynamic Processes



### Section 12.1: Isothermal Process



An isothermal process is a type of thermodynamic process in which the temperature of a system remains constant. This means that the change in temperature, denoted as $\Delta T$, is equal to zero. Isothermal processes are typically observed when a system is in contact with an outside thermal reservoir, allowing for continuous heat exchange to maintain a constant temperature. In contrast, an adiabatic process occurs when a system exchanges no heat with its surroundings.



### Subsection 12.1b: Characteristics of Isothermal Process



An isothermal process is characterized by the following properties:



- Constant temperature: As mentioned, the temperature of the system remains constant throughout the process. This is due to the continuous exchange of heat with an external thermal reservoir.



- Zero change in temperature: The change in temperature, $\Delta T$, is equal to zero. This can be mathematically represented as $\Delta T = 0$.



- Ideal gas behavior: In an isothermal process, the internal energy of an ideal gas remains constant. This is due to the absence of intermolecular forces in ideal gases, which means that the internal energy is solely dependent on temperature.



- Reversible process: An isothermal process is considered to be a reversible process, as it can be reversed without any change in the system or its surroundings.



- Constant pressure: In most cases, an isothermal process occurs at constant pressure. This is because the external thermal reservoir maintains a constant temperature and pressure, allowing for a continuous exchange of heat.



- Idealized conditions: Isothermal processes are often used as a starting point for analyzing more complex, non-isothermal processes. This is because they are idealized conditions that can provide a simplified understanding of the behavior of a system.



## Etymology



The term "isothermal" is derived from the Greek words "ἴσος" ("isos") meaning "equal" and "θέρμη" ("therme") meaning "heat". This accurately reflects the nature of an isothermal process, where the temperature remains constant throughout.



## Examples



Isothermal processes can occur in a variety of systems, including highly structured machines and living cells. In fact, some parts of the cycles of heat engines, such as the Carnot cycle, are carried out isothermally. In the thermodynamic analysis of chemical reactions, it is common to first consider the behavior under isothermal conditions and then examine the effects of temperature. Phase changes, such as melting or evaporation, also occur isothermally when they take place at constant pressure. Isothermal processes are often used as a starting point for analyzing more complex, non-isothermal processes.



Ideal gases are of particular interest in isothermal processes due to Joule's second law, which states that the internal energy of an ideal gas is solely dependent on its temperature. This means that in an isothermal process, the internal energy of an ideal gas remains constant. This is a result of the absence of intermolecular forces in ideal gases. It is important to note that this only applies to ideal gases, as the internal energy of liquids is also dependent on pressure.



### Subsection 12.1a: Definition of Isothermal Process



An isothermal process is defined as a thermodynamic process in which the temperature of a system remains constant throughout. This means that the change in temperature, $\Delta T$, is equal to zero. In order for an isothermal process to occur, the system must be in contact with an external thermal reservoir, which allows for a continuous exchange of heat to maintain a constant temperature. This process is often used as a starting point for analyzing more complex, non-isothermal processes. 





# Thermodynamics & Kinetics: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 12: Thermodynamic Processes



### Section 12.1: Isothermal Process



An isothermal process is a type of thermodynamic process in which the temperature of a system remains constant. This means that the change in temperature, denoted as $\Delta T$, is equal to zero. Isothermal processes are typically observed when a system is in contact with an outside thermal reservoir, allowing for continuous heat exchange to maintain a constant temperature. In contrast, an adiabatic process occurs when a system exchanges no heat with its surroundings.



### Subsection 12.1b: Characteristics of Isothermal Process



An isothermal process is characterized by the following properties:



- Constant temperature: As mentioned, the temperature of the system remains constant throughout the process. This is due to the continuous exchange of heat with an external thermal reservoir.



- Zero change in temperature: The change in temperature, $\Delta T$, is equal to zero. This can be mathematically represented as $\Delta T = 0$.



- Ideal gas behavior: In an isothermal process, the internal energy of an ideal gas remains constant. This is due to the absence of intermolecular forces in ideal gases, which means that the internal energy is solely dependent on temperature.



- Reversible process: An isothermal process is considered to be a reversible process, as it can be reversed without any change in the system or its surroundings.



- Constant pressure: In most cases, an isothermal process occurs at constant pressure. This is because the external thermal reservoir maintains a constant temperature and pressure, allowing for a continuous exchange of heat.



- Idealized conditions: Isothermal processes are often used as a starting point for analyzing more complex, non-isothermal processes. This is because they are idealized conditions that can provide a simplified understanding of the behavior of a system.



## Application



The isothermal process has many practical applications in various fields, including chemistry, physics, and engineering. One of the most common applications is in refrigeration systems, where the isothermal process is used to maintain a constant temperature inside the refrigerator. This is achieved by continuously exchanging heat with the external thermal reservoir, which is usually the surrounding air.



Another application of the isothermal process is in the study of ideal gases. As mentioned earlier, an isothermal process is characterized by the constant internal energy of an ideal gas. This property is often used to simplify calculations and analysis of ideal gas behavior in various systems.



## Etymology



The term "isothermal" is derived from the Greek words "iso," meaning equal, and "thermos," meaning heat. It was first used by the French physicist Sadi Carnot in his work on the theory of heat engines in the early 19th century. The concept of an isothermal process was further developed by other scientists, including James Joule and William Thomson (Lord Kelvin), and is now a fundamental concept in thermodynamics.





# Thermodynamics & Kinetics: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 12: Thermodynamic Processes



### Section 12.2: Adiabatic Process



An adiabatic process is a type of thermodynamic process that occurs without transferring heat or mass between the thermodynamic system and its environment. This means that the change in internal energy, denoted as $\Delta U$, is equal to zero. Unlike an isothermal process, an adiabatic process transfers energy to the surroundings only as work. As a key concept in thermodynamics, the adiabatic process supports the theory that explains the first law of thermodynamics.



### Subsection 12.2a: Definition of Adiabatic Process



The term "adiabatic" comes from the Greek word "adiábatos", meaning "impassable". This refers to the fact that in an adiabatic process, there is no transfer of heat between the system and its surroundings. This can be mathematically represented as $\delta Q = 0$, where $\delta Q$ is the heat transfer.



In an adiabatic process, the change in internal energy is solely dependent on the work done by the system, denoted as $\delta W$. This can be expressed using the first law of thermodynamics as:



$$

\Delta U = \delta Q + \delta W = 0

$$



Since there is no heat transfer, the work done by the system is equal to the change in internal energy. This means that the work done is solely responsible for any change in the system's energy.



### Derivation of "P"–"V" relation for adiabatic heating and cooling



To further understand the behavior of an adiabatic process, we can derive the "P"-"V" relation for adiabatic heating and cooling. Starting with the first law of thermodynamics:



$$

dU + \delta W = \delta Q = 0

$$



We can substitute the expression for work done by the system, $\delta W = P \, dV$, to get:



$$

dU + P \, dV = 0

$$



Since the internal energy is a function of temperature and volume, we can express it as $dU = \left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial T}\right)_V dT + \left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial V}\right)_T dV$. Substituting this into the previous equation, we get:



$$

\left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial T}\right)_V dT + \left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial V}\right)_T dV + P \, dV = 0

$$



Rearranging and simplifying, we get:



$$

\left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial T}\right)_V dT = -\left[P + \left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial V}\right)_T\right] dV

$$



Since the process is adiabatic, the change in internal energy is equal to zero, $\Delta U = 0$. This means that $\left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial T}\right)_V = 0$. Substituting this into the previous equation, we get:



$$

0 = -\left[P + \left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial V}\right)_T\right] dV

$$



Solving for pressure, we get:



$$

P = -\left(\frac{\partial U}{\partial V}\right)_T

$$



This is known as the "P"-"V" relation for adiabatic processes. It shows that the pressure is solely dependent on the change in internal energy with respect to volume at constant temperature.



### Characteristics of Adiabatic Process



An adiabatic process is characterized by the following properties:



- No heat transfer: As mentioned, an adiabatic process does not involve any heat transfer between the system and its surroundings. This means that the temperature of the system remains constant throughout the process.



- Change in internal energy: The change in internal energy, $\Delta U$, is equal to zero. This means that the work done by the system is solely responsible for any change in energy.



- Reversible process: An adiabatic process is considered to be a reversible process, as it can be reversed without any change in the system or its surroundings.



- Ideal gas behavior: In an adiabatic process, the internal energy of an ideal gas remains constant. This is due to the absence of intermolecular forces in ideal gases, which means that the internal energy is solely dependent on temperature.



- Constant entropy: In an adiabatic process, the entropy of the system remains constant. This is because there is no heat transfer, which is the driving force for changes in entropy.



- Adiabatic approximation: Some chemical and physical processes occur too rapidly for energy to enter or leave the system as heat. In these cases, the adiabatic approximation can be used to simplify the analysis of the process.



## Application



The adiabatic process is commonly used in various fields, including chemistry, physics, and meteorology. In chemistry, it is used to study the behavior of chemical reactions that occur without any heat transfer. In physics, it is used to analyze the behavior of systems that do not exchange heat with their surroundings. In meteorology, it is used to explain the formation of clouds and precipitation through adiabatic cooling and heating.





# Thermodynamics & Kinetics: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 12: Thermodynamic Processes



### Section 12.2: Adiabatic Process



An adiabatic process is a type of thermodynamic process that occurs without transferring heat or mass between the thermodynamic system and its environment. This means that the change in internal energy, denoted as $\Delta U$, is equal to zero. Unlike an isothermal process, an adiabatic process transfers energy to the surroundings only as work. As a key concept in thermodynamics, the adiabatic process supports the theory that explains the first law of thermodynamics.



### Subsection 12.2a: Definition of Adiabatic Process



The term "adiabatic" comes from the Greek word "adiábatos", meaning "impassable". This refers to the fact that in an adiabatic process, there is no transfer of heat between the system and its surroundings. This can be mathematically represented as $\delta Q = 0$, where $\delta Q$ is the heat transfer.



In an adiabatic process, the change in internal energy is solely dependent on the work done by the system, denoted as $\delta W$. This can be expressed using the first law of thermodynamics as:



$$

\Delta U = \delta Q + \delta W = 0

$$



Since there is no heat transfer, the work done by the system is equal to the change in internal energy. This means that the work done is solely responsible for any change in the system's energy.



### Subsection 12.2b: Characteristics of Adiabatic Process



There are several key characteristics of an adiabatic process that make it distinct from other thermodynamic processes. These include:



- No heat transfer: As mentioned before, an adiabatic process does not involve any heat transfer between the system and its surroundings. This is due to the fact that the system is insulated and does not allow for any exchange of heat.

- Reversible or irreversible: An adiabatic process can be either reversible or irreversible. In a reversible adiabatic process, the system is able to return to its initial state after the process is completed. In an irreversible adiabatic process, the system is unable to return to its initial state and some energy is lost to the surroundings.

- Constant entropy: In an adiabatic process, the entropy of the system remains constant. This is because there is no heat transfer, which is the driving force for changes in entropy.

- Work done by the system: As mentioned before, the work done by the system is solely responsible for any change in the system's energy. This work can be in the form of mechanical work, electrical work, or any other type of work.

- Adiabatic expansion and compression: Adiabatic processes can involve either expansion or compression of the system. In an adiabatic expansion, the system does work on the surroundings and its internal energy decreases. In an adiabatic compression, the surroundings do work on the system and its internal energy increases.



Overall, the adiabatic process is an important concept in thermodynamics and is used to understand the behavior of systems that do not exchange heat with their surroundings. It is also a key component in the first law of thermodynamics, which states that energy cannot be created or destroyed, only transferred or converted from one form to another. 





# Thermodynamics & Kinetics: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 12: Thermodynamic Processes



### Section 12.2: Adiabatic Process



An adiabatic process is a type of thermodynamic process that occurs without transferring heat or mass between the thermodynamic system and its environment. This means that the change in internal energy, denoted as $\Delta U$, is equal to zero. Unlike an isothermal process, an adiabatic process transfers energy to the surroundings only as work. As a key concept in thermodynamics, the adiabatic process supports the theory that explains the first law of thermodynamics.



### Subsection 12.2a: Definition of Adiabatic Process



The term "adiabatic" comes from the Greek word "adiábatos", meaning "impassable". This refers to the fact that in an adiabatic process, there is no transfer of heat between the system and its surroundings. This can be mathematically represented as $\delta Q = 0$, where $\delta Q$ is the heat transfer.



In an adiabatic process, the change in internal energy is solely dependent on the work done by the system, denoted as $\delta W$. This can be expressed using the first law of thermodynamics as:



$$

\Delta U = \delta Q + \delta W = 0

$$



Since there is no heat transfer, the work done by the system is equal to the change in internal energy. This means that the work done is solely responsible for any change in the system's energy.



### Subsection 12.2b: Characteristics of Adiabatic Process



There are several key characteristics of an adiabatic process that make it distinct from other thermodynamic processes. These include:



- No heat transfer: As mentioned before, an adiabatic process does not involve any heat transfer between the system and its surroundings. This is due to the fact that the system is insulated and does not allow for any exchange of heat.

- Reversible or irreversible: An adiabatic process can be either reversible or irreversible. In a reversible adiabatic process, the system is able to return to its initial state after the process is reversed. This means that the work done by the system is equal to the work done on the system. In an irreversible adiabatic process, the system is unable to return to its initial state and the work done on the system is greater than the work done by the system.

- Constant entropy: In an adiabatic process, the entropy of the system remains constant. This is because there is no heat transfer, which is the driving force for changes in entropy. Therefore, the change in entropy, denoted as $\Delta S$, is equal to zero.

- Adiabatic expansion and compression: Adiabatic processes are commonly seen in the expansion and compression of gases. In an adiabatic expansion, the gas expands without any heat transfer, causing its temperature to decrease. In an adiabatic compression, the gas is compressed without any heat transfer, causing its temperature to increase. This is known as the adiabatic cooling and heating processes, respectively.



### Subsection 12.2c: Role in Thermodynamics



The adiabatic process plays a crucial role in thermodynamics, particularly in the first law of thermodynamics. As mentioned before, the first law states that the change in internal energy of a system is equal to the work done on the system. In an adiabatic process, where there is no heat transfer, the change in internal energy is solely dependent on the work done by the system. This helps to reinforce the concept of energy conservation in thermodynamics.



Furthermore, the adiabatic process is also important in understanding the second law of thermodynamics. The second law states that the total entropy of a closed system always increases over time. In an adiabatic process, where the entropy remains constant, it can be seen as an idealized process that approaches the reversible limit. This helps to illustrate the concept of entropy and its relationship to energy transfer in thermodynamic systems.



In practical applications, the adiabatic process is used in various industries, such as refrigeration and air conditioning, to control the transfer of heat and energy. It is also used in the study of fluid dynamics, where the adiabatic process is used to analyze the behavior of fluids in different conditions.



Overall, the adiabatic process is a fundamental concept in thermodynamics that helps to explain the behavior of energy and entropy in thermodynamic systems. Its role in both the first and second laws of thermodynamics makes it a crucial topic for understanding the principles of thermodynamics. 





# Thermodynamics & Kinetics: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 12: Thermodynamic Processes



### Section 12.3: Isobaric Process



An isobaric process is a type of thermodynamic process in which the pressure of the system remains constant. This means that the change in pressure, denoted as $\Delta P$, is equal to zero. In an isobaric process, the system is allowed to exchange heat and work with its surroundings, while maintaining a constant pressure.



### Subsection 12.3a: Definition of Isobaric Process



The term "isobaric" comes from the Greek words "isos" meaning "equal" and "baros" meaning "weight". This refers to the fact that in an isobaric process, the pressure remains constant, thus the weight of the system also remains constant. This can be mathematically represented as $\Delta P = 0$, where $\Delta P$ is the change in pressure.



In an isobaric process, the change in internal energy, denoted as $\Delta U$, is dependent on both the heat transfer, denoted as $\delta Q$, and the work done, denoted as $\delta W$. This can be expressed using the first law of thermodynamics as:



$$

\Delta U = \delta Q + \delta W

$$



Since the pressure remains constant, the work done by the system can be expressed as:



$$

\delta W = P\Delta V

$$



where $P$ is the constant pressure and $\Delta V$ is the change in volume. Substituting this into the first law of thermodynamics equation, we get:



$$

\Delta U = \delta Q + P\Delta V

$$



### Subsection 12.3b: Characteristics of Isobaric Process



There are several key characteristics of an isobaric process that make it distinct from other thermodynamic processes. These include:



- Constant pressure: As mentioned before, an isobaric process maintains a constant pressure throughout the process. This allows for the system to exchange heat and work with its surroundings without affecting the pressure.

- Work done by the system: In an isobaric process, the work done by the system is equal to the product of the constant pressure and the change in volume. This means that the work done is dependent on the change in volume, rather than the change in pressure.

- Change in internal energy: The change in internal energy in an isobaric process is dependent on both the heat transfer and the work done. This means that the internal energy can change even if the pressure remains constant.

- Ideal gas law: The ideal gas law, which relates the pressure, volume, and temperature of an ideal gas, can be used to calculate the change in internal energy in an isobaric process. This is because the ideal gas law assumes a constant pressure, making it applicable to isobaric processes.





# Thermodynamics & Kinetics: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 12: Thermodynamic Processes



### Section 12.3: Isobaric Process



An isobaric process is a type of thermodynamic process in which the pressure of the system remains constant. This means that the change in pressure, denoted as $\Delta P$, is equal to zero. In an isobaric process, the system is allowed to exchange heat and work with its surroundings, while maintaining a constant pressure.



### Subsection 12.3a: Definition of Isobaric Process



The term "isobaric" comes from the Greek words "isos" meaning "equal" and "baros" meaning "weight". This refers to the fact that in an isobaric process, the pressure remains constant, thus the weight of the system also remains constant. This can be mathematically represented as $\Delta P = 0$, where $\Delta P$ is the change in pressure.



In an isobaric process, the change in internal energy, denoted as $\Delta U$, is dependent on both the heat transfer, denoted as $\delta Q$, and the work done, denoted as $\delta W$. This can be expressed using the first law of thermodynamics as:



$$

\Delta U = \delta Q + \delta W

$$



Since the pressure remains constant, the work done by the system can be expressed as:



$$

\delta W = P\Delta V

$$



where $P$ is the constant pressure and $\Delta V$ is the change in volume. Substituting this into the first law of thermodynamics equation, we get:



$$

\Delta U = \delta Q + P\Delta V

$$



### Subsection 12.3b: Characteristics of Isobaric Process



There are several key characteristics of an isobaric process that make it distinct from other thermodynamic processes. These include:



- Constant pressure: As mentioned before, an isobaric process maintains a constant pressure throughout the process. This allows for the system to exchange heat and work with its surroundings without affecting the pressure.

- Work done by the system: In an isobaric process, the work done by the system is equal to the product of the constant pressure and the change in volume. This means that the work done is directly proportional to the change in volume, and can be positive or negative depending on the direction of the change.

- Change in internal energy: In an isobaric process, the change in internal energy is dependent on the heat transfer and the work done. This means that the internal energy can increase or decrease depending on the direction of the heat transfer and work done.

- Ideal gas law: The ideal gas law, which relates the pressure, volume, and temperature of an ideal gas, can be used to analyze an isobaric process. This is because the pressure remains constant, allowing for the use of the ideal gas law in calculations.

- Molar heat capacity: The molar heat capacity at constant pressure, denoted as $c_P$, is used to calculate the heat transfer in an isobaric process. This is because the heat transfer is directly proportional to the change in temperature, and the molar heat capacity at constant pressure is a measure of how much heat is needed to raise the temperature of a substance by one degree.

- Equipartition theorem: The equipartition theorem, which states that the internal energy of a system is directly proportional to its temperature, can be used to calculate the change in internal energy in an isobaric process. This is because the change in temperature is directly related to the change in internal energy.

## Chapter 12: Thermodynamic Processes

An isobaric process is a type of thermodynamic process in which the pressure of the system remains constant. This means that the change in pressure, denoted as $\Delta P$, is equal to zero. In an isobaric process, the system is allowed to exchange heat and work with its surroundings, while maintaining a constant pressure.



### Subsection 12.3a: Definition of Isobaric Process



The term "isobaric" comes from the Greek words "isos" meaning "equal" and "baros" meaning "weight". This refers to the fact that in an isobaric process, the pressure remains constant, thus the weight of the system also remains constant. This can be mathematically represented as $\Delta P = 0$, where $\Delta P$ is the change in pressure.



In an isobaric process, the change in internal energy, denoted as $\Delta U$, is dependent on both the heat transfer, denoted as $\delta Q$, and the work done, denoted as $\delta W$. This can be expressed using the first law of thermodynamics as:



$$

\Delta U = \delta Q + \delta W

$$



Since the pressure remains constant, the work done by the system can be expressed as:



$$

\delta W = P\Delta V

$$



where $P$ is the constant pressure and $\Delta V$ is the change in volume. Substituting this into the first law of thermodynamics equation, we get:



$$

\Delta U = \delta Q + P\Delta V

$$



### Subsection 12.3b: Characteristics of Isobaric Process



There are several key characteristics of an isobaric process that make it distinct from other thermodynamic processes. These include:



- Constant pressure: As mentioned before, an isobaric process maintains a constant pressure throughout the process. This allows for the system to exchange heat and work with its surroundings without affecting the pressure.

- Work done by the system: In an isobaric process, the work done by the system is equal to the product of the constant pressure and the change in volume. This means that the work done is directly proportional to the change in volume, and can be positive or negative depending on the direction of the change.

- Heat transfer: In an isobaric process, the heat transfer is not affected by the change in volume. This is because the pressure remains constant, so the heat transfer is solely dependent on the change in internal energy.

- Role in thermodynamics: Isobaric processes play an important role in thermodynamics as they allow for the study of systems that exchange heat and work with their surroundings while maintaining a constant pressure. This is particularly useful in understanding the behavior of gases and liquids, as well as in the design and analysis of various industrial processes. Additionally, isobaric processes are often used as a starting point for the study of other thermodynamic processes, such as isothermal and adiabatic processes.





# Thermodynamics & Kinetics: A Comprehensive Guide



## Chapter 12: Thermodynamic Processes



### Section 12.4: Isochoric Process



An isochoric process, also known as a constant-volume process, is a type of thermodynamic process in which the volume of the system remains constant. This means that the change in volume, denoted as $\Delta V$, is equal to zero. In an isochoric process, the system is not allowed to exchange work with its surroundings, but it is allowed to exchange heat.



### Subsection 12.4a: Definition of Isochoric Process



The term "isochoric" comes from the Greek words "isos" meaning "equal" and "khṓros" meaning "space". This refers to the fact that in an isochoric process, the volume remains constant, thus the space occupied by the system also remains constant. This can be mathematically represented as $\Delta V = 0$, where $\Delta V$ is the change in volume.



In an isochoric process, the change in internal energy, denoted as $\Delta U$, is solely dependent on the heat transfer, denoted as $\delta Q$. This can be expressed using the first law of thermodynamics as:



$$

\Delta U = \delta Q

$$



Since the volume remains constant, the work done by the system is equal to zero, as there is no change in volume to perform work on. This means that the first law of thermodynamics can be simplified to:



$$

\Delta U = \delta Q + 0

$$



### Subsection 12.4b: Characteristics of Isochoric Process



There are several key characteristics of an isochoric process that make it distinct from other thermodynamic processes. These include:



- Constant volume: As mentioned before, an isochoric process maintains a constant volume throughout the process. This means that the system cannot perform work on its surroundings, but it can exchange heat.

- No work done by the system: In an isochoric process, the work done by the system is equal to zero, as there is no change in volume to perform work on. This means that all the energy transferred to the system is in the form of heat.

- Quasi-static process: An isochoric process is typically assumed to be a quasi-static process, meaning that it occurs slowly enough for the system to remain in thermal equilibrium at all times. This allows for accurate measurements of temperature and heat transfer.

- Constant pressure: While the pressure is not directly involved in an isochoric process, it is still important to note that the pressure may change due to the exchange of heat. However, this change in pressure does not affect the process itself, as the volume remains constant.

This can be mathematically represented as $\Delta V = 0$, where $\Delta V$ is the change in volume.

$$



### Subsection 12.4b: Characteristics of Isochoric Process



There are several key characteristics of an isochoric process that make it distinct from other thermodynamic processes. These include:

- No work done by the system: In an isochoric process, the work done by the system is equal to zero, as there is no change in volume to perform work on. This means that all the energy transferred to the system is in the form of heat.

- Quasi-static process: An isochoric process is considered to be a quasi-static process, meaning that it occurs slowly enough for the system to remain in thermal equilibrium at all times. This allows for accurate measurements of temperature and pressure to be taken throughout the process.

- Constant pressure: While the volume remains constant, the pressure of the system may change during an isochoric process. This is due to the change in temperature, which affects the pressure of the gas according to the ideal gas law.

- Specific heat capacity at constant volume: The specific heat capacity at constant volume, denoted as $c_v$, is a measure of the amount of heat required to raise the temperature of a unit mass of the substance by one degree while keeping the volume constant. In an isochoric process, the change in internal energy is equal to the heat transfer, so the specific heat capacity at constant volume plays a crucial role in determining the change in temperature of the system.

- Reversible process: An isochoric process is considered to be a reversible process, meaning that it can be reversed by an infinitesimal change in the system's state. This is due to the fact that the system remains in thermal equilibrium throughout the process, allowing for the process to be reversed without any loss of energy.
